They still retain their rabbitness, but you feel empathy for them as you would another human.<br /><br />When his brother Fiver predicts that tragedy is going to befall their warren, Hazel gathers as many people as will go with him to set off for a new place that will be free from danger.  With a small band of rabbits, they face much hardship and dangerous foes in search of a place they can call home.  Eventually, they come to the Watership Downs and find a place high on a hill that proves to be the perfect spot for their new warren.<br /><br />There is one problem however, the only ones who escaped where males.  They need to have some does in order to get on with their lives.  With a daring plan they set out to gather does only to be brutally beaten by a warlord in charge of another warren.  After nearly dying, they decide to devise a plan and get back at this evil foe and make sure that they expand their warren any way possible.<br /><br />Even though they are rabbits, the characters in this novel are amazing and full of depth.  They all have their own distinct personalities but still act like rabbits.  There is no amazing feats of daring that a normal rabbit couldn't do.  They all operate inside their natural limitations.<br /><br />Adams has an easy going writing style.  I found it particularly interesting that he developed his own \\"language\\" for the rabbits.  As a linguist it was interesting to see how he chose to use both the English language and the rabbit's natural along with the pidgin language they used to communicate with other animals.  It showed great complexity.  His writing is very clear and descriptive and he describes the rabbits movements very accurately.  I could picture easily what events were taking place.<br /><br />As a bit of a warning, as much as this could be a book for children, it has some very violent aspects.  The rabbits fight frequently and their injuries are described in detail.  This could be frightening for younger children.  Another part of the book I didn't particularly care for were the stories the rabbits told.  They just seemed like filler and I wasn't that interested in them.<br /><br />A great book to be sure and I can see why it is highly recommended. B004AYCWY4|5.0|When a writer (or an artist) has written their finest work & experienced the adulation & recognition that are beyond their wildest dreams, it is difficult to write a follow-up. Often (as with [[ASIN:B00065XJ52 Encore]] and [[ASIN:B0017WVST2 Long Way Down]]), the follow-up proves weak and attention quickly goes elsewhere.<br /><br />Fortunately this hasn't happened with this book to that degree (although it is a little tired in places - e.g. chapter 8 'You did it yourself in 9 months' and the ending). This book is, like all Dawkins books, an improvement on his last biology book in explaining evolution. Every book since [[ASIN:0192880519 The Extended Phenotype]] has been geared to popularise evolution & help the reader understand it. This book does so very persuasively with incredibly simple arguments in 'The Primrose path to Evolution', leading onto atomic clocks, experiments that have proven evolution and misconceptions about the fossil record.<br /><br />Essentially, if you have a friend who hasn't read Dawkins (shame on them!), then this is a good place to start. Like Ancestor's tale, it makes little mention of religion and God (except Creationists) and is designed to persuade people like the Bishop of Oxford was, who are religious but also believe in evolution. Given the shocking statistic quoted in this book (that 40% of people don't believe in evolution), it is very much your duty to open the doors of perception for these people so that they may see (at least partially) how the World works.<br /><br />That said, this book does have some weaknesses. The digressions Dawkins goes into can sometimes lead off the point & should have been footnoted at the back (as with [[ASIN:0199291152 The Selfish Gene]]). John Cornwell has also criticised Dawkins for quoting his own books and this, unfortunately, is quite common here (as it was with his latest documentary - [[ASIN:B001B8NPL6 The Genius of Charles Darwin]]).<br />One last criticism is that the book [[ASIN:0143116649 Why Evolution Is True]] by Jerry Coyne pops up a little too many times, which makes me wonder if its ideas were lifted for this book. The sources in this book are also not as diverse as some of his other books, and it suffers a little for it.<br /><br />Still, if you can ignore these weaknesses (which are slight), then this is still a good book to persuade those with no scientific knowledge of the truth of evolution. I would suggest, however, that [[ASIN:0753819961 The Ancestor's Tale]] is a more peerless book in this field & I found it much more persuasive even if it is more technical. Knowing the unscholarly as I do, I would suggest getting the audio CD of [[ASIN:0752873210 The Ancestor's Tale]] for those who are unconvinced (since everyone can hear but not all like reading). B003YBNREI|3.0|WORLD GONE CRAZY is being acclaimed as a \\"return to form\\" for the Doobies -- i.e., a return to the pre-Michael McDonald era of THE CAPTAIN AND ME, etc. The truth is that WORLD fits firmly into the *post*-McDonald era of CYCLES, BROTHERHOOD and SIBLING RIVALRY in that it's just a shadow of what the early era produced.    Based on what I've read in recent interviews, the band seems to have decided that the \\"democratic\\" approach used on SIBLING RIVALRY (i.e., all 5 of the core members wrote, and 4 sang lead) was a mistake. Thus they've not only had the usual guys, Tom Johnston & Pat Simmons, do all the singing & writing, but they even seem to have limited those two to one type of song each. Tom only produces basic, good ol' rock 'n' roll pieces (more or less rewrites of songs like \\"The Doctor\\" and \\"Jericho\\"), and Pat only comes up with sappy ballads somewhat reminiscent of \\"Ordinary Man.\\" And that's the real mistake; the variety of SIBLING was hit-and-miss, but it was a far more interesting album than WORLD.    The guys certainly deserve credit for continuing to produce fresh music so long after their debut. But I think they're capable of more than this. There is not one song on WORLD that stands out from the rest in terms of quality, with the possible exception of the nice (if somewhat pointless) remake of 1971's \\"Nobody.\\" On the other hand, no one song stands out as really bad, either (though a couple of the Pat Simmons ballads may come close). And if there's any good news about WORLD, that's it.    The bottom line: If you're enough of a Doobies fan that you listen to, say, BROTHERHOOD, then pick this up. Just don't expect a return to the glory days.    P.S.: The two bonus tracks found on the CD/DVD edition can be easily purchased as downloads, if you just want to get the regular CD version. (Since \\"Law Dogs\\" *stops* rather than *ends* the album, it probably needs those extra tracks to close it.)

0786955570|4.0|Like Pandemic, Shadows over Camelot, and others, this is a cooperative rather than competitive board game. The basic premise is a group of adventurers setting out on a quest, of course, but the unique part of Ravenloft is the way that the monsters work. In a lot of cooperative games the rules lay out the challenge for the players, with little to ability to deviate from a set \\"bad things happen card\\". In this game, each player is periodically given control of a monster with rules to follow, making it less about one person versus the rest (such as with Descent) but still giving flexibility to what the monsters can do. For example, the Gargoyle card is dangerous if it spawns near the group, but if it shows up away it will sit there until they get near, whereas the Wolf enemy will rapidly track down and pounce the adventuring party. This also gives an element of strategy to playing the monsters beneficially to the group, as there are some choices the controller makes, for example if two players are the same distance away, it may be more useful to attack the fighter than the mage.    Not only is Castle Ravenloft a fun, cooperative board game, it's also a good introduction to the D&D 4e rules. Everything is simplified into one kind of hit roll, but the basic \\"roll a 20-sided die and match or exceed\\" is a good teaching tool, and the difference between various kinds of powers meshes with the full game. I could imagine a newbie to tabletop games playing Ravenloft, moving on to the D&D Red Box, then following up with 4e or the Essentials series.

B00000HZH7|5.0|Hard to believe that Primus guitarist/screwball Larry Lalonde, who has dented the collective sanity of all guitar players with cuts like \\"Eleven\\" and \\"Hamburger Train\\" had a hand in this thrash/death classic.  Possessed was the most patentedly Satanic band in the Bay Area - while Slayer used Satanic references strictly for shock value, three of the four members of Possessed walked the walk, with the exception of Lalonde.<br />  But what makes \\"Seven Churches\\" a historical document is the music.  It's said that this CD may very well be the first true death metal album, and based on the barking dog vocals and over-the-top lyrical content, it may be true, or at least be one of the very first.  Possessed were not a slow sludgy outfit like their Floridian kin Obituary or the legendary Morbid Angel.  \\"Seven Churches\\" cruises along at high speed, reminding one of other early thrash greats like Exodus' debut \\"Bonded By Blood\\" and Kreator's \\"Pleasure To Kill\\", another historically important thrash/death hybrid.<br />  People put off by the overdone Satanic shtick should just ignore the vocals since they're hard to understand anyway, but relish the power and energy this band had in its short life span.  Maybe Possessed were too heavy too soon, but the fact is that \\"Seven Churches\\" is a must have for extreme metal fans, and important for its historical value.  A rather poor production, but still a vicious piece of work.

B000ESST6U|5.0|I checked this DVD out from the library a couple times, and loved it so much I just had to buy it on Amazon. While I'm familar with many of Handel's oratorios, this is the first of his operas I've heard. Thankfully, the music is every bit as beautiful and inventive as Handel's oratorios. While virtually lacking in choruses, Giulio Cesare makes up for it by a broader range of arias for most characters. Cleopatra and Caesar, for example, thus come alive as complex characters to a much further extent than (say) those in Saul. One vivdly feels their emotional highs and lows as they progress through the story.    The orchestra deliver a vibrant period-instrument style performance throughout, and all the singers are excellent as well. The production is set in relatively modern times (the Romans resembling British redcoats), and yet the iconic looks associated with Caesar and Cleopatra are fused seamlessly and powerfully into the 20th Century setting. I also found the oft-mentioned \\"dance routines\\" highly effective. They keep things entertaining to look at during the arias, and help accentuate the dance rhythms so common in Baroque music. Watching the DVD, it's obvious how much fun both the audiences and performers were having with this production, and that spirit translates well to the home release.    Overall, to anyone who likes Handel's oratorios, I highly recommend both \\"Giulio Caesar\\" in general, and this performance in particular. This was my first foray into Handel's operas, but it definitely won't be the last.

B003ZJJFKE|5.0|Welp i've been a Satch fan ever since i heard Surfing With An Alien back in 87 when i was around 12 or so, my cousin turned me onto it, and i was like wow this dude shreds. It wasn't until 89's Flying In A Blue Dream that i really stood up and took notice of how talented Joe really was and how hard his stuff was to play(or attempt). The main thing about Joe is he is a song writer, not just a shredder who puts down some mundane or mediocre tracks that all follow the same routine and all sound alike. Sure you have incredible players like Petrucci, Loomis, Vinnie Moore, Paul Gilbert etc. But they just don't have what Joe has, that style. When he plucks the string you know its him, and also he doesnt go by any book and follow routine after routine with six string sweeps, special phrasing, and certain modes. Joe is an entity all his own, just like Vai who i should mention as well, thats why they are the two biggest solo guitarist(all guitar) of all time. Well getting down to Black Swans and Wormhole Wizards, Joe goes back to some old roots while keeping the fresh watered ones alive.    This album is packed with very versatile material as most of his albums are. Premonition kicks off with that good ole Joe styled low distorted riff that we all know and loves while skewing into a nice chorus that reminds you of stuff off of Crystal Planet, a lot. A lot of the album follows that route along with some new things. The half-title track Wormhole Wizards is awesome, very creepy traveling through space like intro, with a great solid minor bass line that follows through the whole song without change. Making it a very good rythm section for the song. Also in this song along with a few others there are keyboards in the back, and actually on the fore mentioned track there is a keyboard solo, a first for a Joe Record? Perhaps. All the tracks are great and have there own style, as usual. But all the while you listen you know its Joe. The great thing about his playing and what has always made it so difficult to mimic(though semi-duplicated, but not spot on) is the way he uses arpeggios, and his use of hard/soft/and no picking, which is something lots of players try but it ends up sounding like crap. Listen to George Lynch you'll see what i mean.    So i can't pick just one song to sit and talk about although i think my favorite are the two i mentioned along with, God Is Crying, Dream Song, The Golden Room, and pyrrhic victoria(whatever that means)! I should also mention that God Is Crying is a really good throw back to old school Joe and something off the Extremist. Most songs do sound like they are from the Crystal Planet era and some even sound like a \\"better\\" version of Engines Of Creation, which wasn't bad just different. So if you like good ole Joe pick this up, its about time some originality came back to guitar playing, and Joe just has those songs that you can hum and really get stuck in your head. I really like Vai, but he really hasn't done much worth in the last decade or so, mostly experimental stuff, and not an album of really catchy songs like Passion And Warfare which was no doubt his best. So how does this rate, well along with the earlier albums its right next to Is There Love In Space, which was his best album in the last ten years, so tack this one on as well. The others were great but didn't seem to have \\"as much\\" catchy material, except for maybe Crowd Chant, Redshift Riders, Theme For A Strange World and Andalusia, which were some of my favorites from the last albums. Joe has never had a \\"bad\\" album that you could say wow this sucks, he's just had some records that were a little uninspired perhaps. But make no mistake he has broken that trend with Black Swans and Wormhole Wizards. Also the title track Wormhole Wizards, is without the Black Swans, i really thought there would be a song called that, whats up with that Joe? :) Enjoy.!

And with good reason.  First, a single play-through of the record revealed that John's songs were relatively weak.  He'd been hanging out inside the Dakota smoking bong hits for years, not even bothering to pick up a guitar, and it showed.  The session players were energetic, but John's melodies and lyrics were sub-par.  And then there was the issue of having the album appear in the form of a \\"dialogue\\" between John and Yoko.  It's a fanciful idea, but all it really did was keep people from playing the John side and leaving the Yoko side silent.  Her songs were typically polarizing.  And in all honesty, has there ever been a more over-praised artist in history than Yoko Ono?  If John Lennon hadn't fallen in love with her she'd be nowhere.  Certainly not a musician.  A minor art world figure at best.      The album debuted at #27 in the UK, reached as high as #14, but then it fell down very quickly to #25 and then #46. In the US, the album debuted at #25 and remained out of the top 10.  But then, of course, after John was killed Double Fantasy started hopping off the shelves and we were inundated with second-rate songs like (Just Like) Starting Over and the smug, self-aggrandizing Watching the Wheels on the radio.  Everyone felt the need to be kind to the record, which now took on this totally unjustified significance.  But the fact that John died just after its release is irrelevant.  Stripped or Un-Stripped DOUBLE FANTASY is a tame, lame rock & roll record - musically unadventurous and lyrically sedate - and just about the last thing I would ever listen to if I wanted to hear some great John Lennon music.

B003ZJ0ZX0|4.0|Bruno Mars certainly had one of the more anticipated debut albums of 2010.  After superb turns with other artists (\\"Nothin' On You\\" with B.o.B and \\"Billionaire\\" with Travie McCoy), Mars struck out on his own with his solo #1 hit, \\"Just the Way You Are.\\" Mars's debut is not as perfect as the Billy Joel lifted title, but [[ASIN:B003ZJ0ZX0 Doo - Wops & Hooligans]] possesses more than enough solid moments over it short ten tracks at thirty-five minutes total to be both enjoyable and promising for Mars's future.  Hopefully, Doo-Wops & Hooligans strikes more a chord than some recent albums by debut artists, namely the dismal selling, though enjoyable[[ASIN:B003R4JB56 31 Minutes to Takeoff]] by soulful pop singer Mike Posner.  Additionally, let's hope his recent run-in with the law (a felony) does not degrade a promising career.<br /><br />\\"Grenade\\" opens the album off a bit oddly, finding Mars contrasting his usually polished, lazy vocals with more overt, forced vocals.  \\"Grenade\\" sounds the slightest bit clunky at times and does not represent Mars as the crooner that we've (the listeners) have come to know him as.  He has some nice vocal moments, achieving some soulful grit that listeners have also desired to hear, but he overdoes the nuances vocally.  \\"Grenade\\" does grow on you after a couple of listens, but despite its sound production work, it is eclipsed by much better cuts from 'Doo-Wops & Hooligans.' Think of \\"Grenade\\" as a bit of `false-start.'<br /><br />\\"Just The Way You Are\\" atones for any questions of Mars's credibility that may be raised from the left-field \\"Grenade.\\" Here the songwriting is much better executed and the production work is simple, yet beautiful and captivating.  \\"Just the Way You Are\\" is the valedictory cut by all means, accentuated by a schmaltzy, yet sincere refrain: \\"when I see your face, there's not a thing that I would change, cause you're amazing, just the way you are...\\"  \\"Our First Time\\" rivals the greatness of \\"Just the Way You Are,\\" only missing its superbness by a few short notches.  Initially, the reggae-tinged cut may be a bit `off-putting,' but quickly one feels the lazy vibe suits Mars perfectly.  Again, the songwriting is strong with another catchy, though not revolutionary hook (\\"just go wit it... and I will go real slow wit it... it's our first time\\").  But let's face it, the charm of Mars's vocal makes up for the corniness right?<br /><br />\\"Runaway Baby\\" is like \\"Grenade\\" in the sense that it is a bit awkward.  Mars thrives on lazier cuts as opposed to up-tempo numbers that degrade his vocal polish slightly.  But he once again atones for this with a the enjoyable and lyrically relevant \\"Lazy Song\\" which finds  Mars referencing the rap song \\"Teach Me How To Dougie,\\" among other absurdities.  \\"Lazy Song\\" is enjoyable because of Mars' consummate pop culture references and `stream of consciousness' approach in addition to it brief 3:20 length.<br /><br />\\"Marry You\\" is solid, though it sounds similar in conception and sound to \\"Just the Way You Are,\\" lifting the underlying progression.  Despite this, the production is clever, the drums are big sounding quoting the 1980s, and the corny `wedding bells' are actually a clever addition.  \\"Talking to the Moon\\" slows the tempo once more, showing off the beauty of Mars's vocals.  The production is solid and while \\"Talking to the Moon\\" also sounds similar to previous cuts (namely the progression is similar to \\"Billionaire\\"), Mars pulls it off incredibly well.  It is another nice addition to the track list.<br /><br />\\"Liquor Store Blues,\\" featuring Damian Marley is as enjoyable as everything else here.  Again, Mars favors a reggae-tinged approach, but it bodes well for him.  The hook is clever once again: \\"one shot for my pain, one drag for my sorrow, get messed up today, I'll be ok tomorrow.\\"  Marley is surprisingly tamer than usual here, not stealing Mars's thunder in the least.  \\"Count On Me\\" finds Mars sounding like a mix between Jason Mraz and Jack Johnson; the cut itself channels recent Plain White T's hit single \\"1,2,3,4\\".  There is nothing monumental here, but some smart choices are made to omit drums from the production work and add nice, lush strings.  Closing cut \\"The Other Side,\\" featuring Cee-Lo Green and B.o.B is another standout, and the perfect choice to close this effort.  It is uptempo, but Mars sounds better than previous uptempo cuts, though it may be Cee-Lo Green that steals the show.  B.o.B.'s verse is strong, additionally.<br /><br />Overall, this is a solid debut album on all accounts.  It is short, but given the fact that Mars' relies on lazy grooves for his best vocal performances, the short length bodes in his favor;  a longer album would have knocked the quality down a notch most likely.  Vocally, there is room for more maturity for Mars, but if given the chance, I think that we as the listeners can expect it on his NEXT album.  Strong debut; 3 1/2 starsannels recent Plain White T's hit single \\"1,2,3,4\\". It is uptempo, but Mars sounds better than previous uptempo cuts, though it may be Cee-Lo Green that steals the show.  B.o.B.'s verse is strong, additionally.      Overall, this is a solid debut album on all accounts.  It is short, but given the fact that Mars' relies on lazy grooves for his best vocal performances, the short length bodes in his favor;  a longer album would have knocked the quality down a notch most likely. B00008CLOA|5.0|One fine day in March 1973, as a fresh-faced teen I bought my vinyl copy of DSOTM on its release-day from Virgin Records in Liverpool. I still have that vinyl album in its original first-day sleeve, lined up in order (in that anally-retentive way men often have with cataloguing their music collections) between my original vinyl first-day releases of 'Obscured by Clouds' and 'Wish You Were Here.'<br /><br />By the fifth or sixth bar of 'The Great Gig in the Sky' it became obvious that Floyd had produced a more `commercial' mainstream album, more accessible to the mainstream. I even thought people might still be listening to it in 10 years' time - in the far future in 1983 when surely half of us would be actually living in geodetic domes on the dark side of The Moon. I witnessed live performances by the band at the old Rainbow Theatre in Finsbury Park, at Earl's Court and at Knebworth on a beautiful summer evening.<br /><br />Now 37 years later, new generations are still discovering this timeless classic from the pre-internet, pre-PC prog-rock era. If you are one of the small percentage of the global population who have never heard this epic piece and you don't know what all the fuss is about then go buy, rent or borrow the album. If you can't find an original UK Harvest-label CD pressing from the 1980s, listen to a SACD disk on a dedicated SACD player to experience the deep, rich, beautiful sound achieved by Alan Parsons' exemplary production. You'll soon understand why this music has outlasted just about everything else recorded in the 1970s, 80s and 90s (anyone remember 'Punk' from the late 1970s? Anyone listening to it now? No). Listen to the album as a whole, from start to finish, and appreciate the way the tracks seamlessly flow from one to the next as they explore the pressures faced by any self-aware human living in modern society and the ever-present risk of it all driving you nuts.<br /><br />In finding ever-newer generations of appreciative listeners, DSOTM has somehow transcended `rock' and become a kind of classical music. It sounds fresher and more contemporary than most music from the 80s and 90s, which often now sounds dated. It's Pink Floyd at the high point of their long, chequered and mould-breaking career as cerebral, creative, experimental, intelligent originals, full of beautiful melodies, subtlety of light and shade, major-to-minor key changes, inventive use of instrumentation and sound, rich complexity, lyrical and poetic writing, real-life speech samples (Roger Waters' idea, a quirky stroke of genius which works and adds to the album's unique character), and a couple of hit singles thrown in. It's intelligent, serious in theme yet often humorous. The music is absolutely first class and there is not one minute of playing time where it's less than superb: it's the ultimate `concept album', a milestone of excellence; the musical signature of a generation, of its time and yet for all time, and you can listen to it forever.<br /><br />In fact, if you don't have DSOTM in your collection then you're probably not serious about music. And this is the version to have.nded `rock' and become a kind of classical music. It sounds fresher and more contemporary than most music from the 80s and 90s, which often now sounds dated. It's intelligent, serious in theme yet often humorous. The music is absolutely first class and there is not one minute of playing time where it's less than superb: it's the ultimate `concept album', a milestone of excellence; the musical signature of a generation, of its time and yet for all time, and you can listen to it forever.    In fact, if you don't have DSOTM in your collection then you're probably not serious about music. And this is the version to have.
B003MTGW80|4.0|Giulini arrived in Los Angeles in 1978, at the age of 64, but he had already entered his late phase, which was slow, restrained, and wise. He stayed for six seasons, producing recordings for DG and worshipful praise from almost all quarters.  The orchestra itself was not a great one. For a long time there had been a gap between L.A.'s wealth of Hollywood free lances and the generally mediocre local orchestra.  Optimistically Giulini noted that the orchestra, which had much improved under Mehta, was full of young musicians eager to learn more.  Mehta had also given the LAPO an aura of glamour and a healthy recording contract with Decca. DG was not as generous in its output. In this bargain box set we get 6 CDs taped between Nov. 1978 and Nov. 1981 -- presumably the lack of public or more likely financial support led to no more recordings.<br /><br />Up to this point I only knew a handful of the original recordings, my favorites being the Beethoven \\"Eroica\\" and Schumann \\"Rhenish.\\"  Since this is new territory for me, I'll just give my impressions of each disc on first listen.<br /><br />CD 1<br />The liner notes make an apt comparison between Giulini's broad pacing and reading a Victorian novel. If you want an ultra-dynamic or fervent reading of the Eroica, his 1978 version won't satisfy--at 20 min., the first movement is considerably slower than the norm, even for the era before period style sped things up. But there's not a boring note here. Giulini imparts expressivity to this music in such a way that you believe in his viewpoint, which is romantic, inward and personal. The musicians are right in step, and DG's recording has a full, warm bloom about it.  The filler is Schumann's Manfred Over. from 1981, performed convincingly in the same style, aiming for depth rather than dynamic propulsion.<br /><br />CD 2<br />This pairing of the Beethoven Fifth and \\"Pastoral\\" has the same weight as the \\"Eroica\\" -- it dates from a year later -- and although tempos are not extreme, the overall energy level in the Fifth can't be compared to Carlos Kleiber's famous version.  Phrasing is fairly generalized and reflective in mood. the finale bursts with power and grandeur, again at a broad pace a la Klemperer or Furtwangler. the same is true for the \\"Pastoral,\\" which like giulini's other two versions that I know (from London and La Scala) could easily be mistaken for one from a German conductor of the old school. It is saved from Kapellmeister limbo by its careful, at times loving phrasing. If you appreciate Gunter Wand's Beethoven, tis reading will be more appealing than it was to me.<br /><br />CD 3<br />I know of three stereo recordings of the Brahms First by Giulini, all of which follow the same epic contour.  The one with the Vienna Phil. displays more power and virtuosity; the one with the Bavarian Radio SO, which dates from 1979, two years before this L.A. recording, is one for the ages -- noble, strong, tragic in all its dimensions. All of those virtues exist here but in smaller quantity, with less tension and impact.  I'd call tis the least of the three choices.<br /><br />CD 4<br />Buyers may grumble at a 48 min. CD filled with only the Brahms Second, but DG got into the habit of releasing very stingy discs with Giulini, highlighting them as events much of the time. I know of one other version, with the Vienna Phil., which seemed too reflective and inward--it was more like a meditation on the Brahms Second.  The L.A. alternative is no different in its broadness and subdued energy. The finale comes alive with a faster tempo' on both instances you need to be in a Celibidache mood to really tune in.<br /><br />CD 5<br />This disc begins with a Schumann \\"Rhenish\\" from 1979 that I have loved for a long time. It's almost stately, in the manner of Klemperer's Schumann, but with less grit and more tenderness.  The orchestra seems more energized than in Giulini's Beethoven and Brahms, but not by much. They gave him a consistently deep-voiced sound without an emphasis on momentum. I regret using the same adjectives so often; although cut from the same cloth, the conductor did try to make each reading personal. From the same sessions comes a Tchaikovsky \\"Pathetique\\" that plays to strength. I think about it in the same way as I thought about his 1959 recording on EMI with the Philharmonia: Giulinie can't match the incandescence of Mravinsky, the sheer virtuosity of Reiner with the Chicago Sym., or the personal intensity of Bernstein on DG, but his is an ardent, heart-on-sleeve performance that appeals to me far more than, say, Jansons or Pletnev in their cooler, more streamlined approach.  By the time he came to L.A., the interpretation was more reserved but just as personal. You may miss the earlier dash, but in exchange the recorded sound is much better.<br /><br />CD 6<br />This is a French program pairing Debussy (La Mer) and Ravel (Ma Mere l'Oye, Rapsodie espagnole), with the style being as warm and relaxed as everywhere else. But here I find that more magic occurs. Giulini's earlier Ravel and Debussy on EMI, with the first orchestra he made his name with, the Philharmonia, earned much praise. Here in L.A., with considerably better sound, the atmosphere he weaves is more delicate and entrancing.  I would call these the truly memorable items in this collection, worth seeking out on their own, along with the Schumann \\"Rhenish,\\" if you don't want to acquire the entire set.ound without an emphasis on momentum. I regret using the same adjectives so often; although cut from the same cloth, the conductor did try to make each reading personal. 1439148953|5.0|Eilis Lacey, the protagonist in Colm Toibin's novel \\"Brooklyn\\" is a totally unremarkable girl growing into womanhood surrounded by all too-ordinary family and friends. It is perhaps the ordinariness of Eilis and the things that happen to her that prove author Toibin's mastery of human emotion and the language that endears her to us.<br /><br />Growing up in a small Irish village, her concerns are those of all young women from poor families - she knows very little of the world beyond her small town, her mother, and her more mature and \\"smart\\" older sister. She is lucky to have a job in an upscale food shop ruled by an affected martinet, to benefit from her sister's counsel, and the influence of a friendly parish priest. Indeed, it is Father Flood, a kindly American priest, who jumpstarts her life with the fare for passage to America, a place to live, and a place to work in Brooklyn, USA.<br /><br />These early sectors of the book portray Ms. Lacey as she is, rustic, uneducated, unsophisticated, yet yearning for a better life and the ambition to work hard to pursue an education. Along the way, she falls in love and secretly marries an American-Italian, returns to Ireland to her mother following her sister's death. There, she must choose between a new would-be love and her secret husband in America.<br /><br />Beyond the plot which is superbly worked out, none of it could matter were it not for author Toibin's fine scalpel with the language of character development. His prose is lean - I dare you to look at the book and try to change a single word in a single sentence. And as an Irishman, too, I can hear the hush of soft Irish accent in the dialogue. \\"Brooklyn\\" raises the bar for character development. If you like character-driven novels, this is one you can't afford to miss.

Blind Guardian was my second-ever metal band in 2002 with the album Night At the Opera (2002; band's 3rd most recent album) and a couple of months later picked up Nightfall in Middle Earth (1998; band's 4th most recent).  Now I have this.  I've heard a small handful of cover songs from earlier works and my favorite Blind Guardian song is the colossal And Then There Was Silence.  I listened to the singles off of 2006's A Twist in the Myth when they were first put online, but felt that Blind Guardian did not transition to Nuclear Blast very well.  All said and done, Blind Guardian is \\"familiar\\" to me:  I'm rejoiced to hear Hansi's voice (and actually bought 01011001 because I heard he was on it), I like fantasy metal (see my review on Rhapsody of Fire's The Frozen Tears of Angels), and I'm a sucker for lots of effort going into every second of an album, especially when the album is over an hour (take Heavenly's Dust to Dust - over 70 minutes - for example).      Blind Guardian introduces an orchestra on this, their second album with Nuclear Blast.  It is primarily used in two songs (first and last).  I commend Blind Guardian for this.  I will use Nightwish (my +first+ ever metal band) and their albums Once and Dark Passion Play for examples:  I look at the band's inclusion of an orchestra as the replacing of Tuomas's trademark keyboards.  While most people have a hard time with Dark Passion Play because they can't get over Nightwish firing founding member and vocalist Tarja Turunen, I think it's because Tuomas toned down keyboard inclusion that I feel made Oceanborn and Wishmaster the truly memorable albums they are.  Blind Guardian makes no such sacrifice to their trademark sound, or, if they do, there still are 8 (or 4/5 of the) songs to fall back on.      Blind Guardian is a band of bards.  Of all of the bands in the power metal category I can think of featuring so many lyrics in the lyrics-to-song-length ratio, only Sonata Arctica rivals Blind Guardian.  With so many lyrics in each song, they deserve quite a bit of attention.  Both Blind Guardian and Sonata Arctica are at the bottom of lists of bands you'd expect to hear several minute-and-a-half instrumental intros to songs, minute-and-a-half solos, etc.  With Blind Guardian, I have always felt that the instruments follow the vocals/lyrics.  I would guess that the lyrics are conceived first and then appropriately accompanying music is thought up later.  So, if you have a distaste for the vocals of Hansi Kursch, I'd bet you have no chance at enjoying Blind Guardian.  I also imagine that Sonata Arctica and Blind Guardian must share more of the same bands than most other pairs of power metal bands we could suggest.      Getting more specific on the subject on lyrics, we again do not have the Tolkien-inspired lyrics we had on so many of the early Blind Guardian albums.  We don't have numerous characters (except in The Road of No Release).  We don't have songs that flow into one another.  We don't have sound effects for swords clashing, trekking through forests, entering a wizard's lair, etc.  We +do+ have the Blind Guardian-standard exceptionally complete songs, that is, no song on this album leaves me feeling \\"dang, I wish they had explored this idea further.\\"      Ideas vary.  I wish the lyric book included what each song is based on.  Sacred Worlds is the most macrocosmic song I have heard by Blind Guardian and, recalling comparisons to Sonata Arctica, compares best to The Power of One, their longest song.  I would venture to say that each and every one of the 6 billion-plus people we have on Earth would get something different out of these songs and that each one of them +can+ find something of meaning in these songs.  During my first listen of Sacred Worlds, I said to myself, \\"... um... where is the Blind Guardian sound?\\" but it serves that purpose Nuclear Blast seems to be so fond of of having their bands reaching out to audiences previously ignored.  Valkyries stands out on the album as it is concerned with Vikings unlike any other song on the album (at least that I've noticed so far).  This song definitely makes me stop and ask myself if Blind Guardian forgot that they are not from Scandinavia when they wrote this one.  The aforementioned Road of No Release will appeal to storyteller listener as we have a whopping six characters (all sung by Hansi Kursch).  Ride Into Obsession strikes me as the most aggressive song on the album:  it's chaotic.  This song and the last one both deal with Robert Jordan's Wheel of Time novel series.  In fact, just about all if not all of the songs on the album have roots in literature.  The album ends with Wheel of Time, perhaps the song that encompasses all of what the album presents in its first 9 songs, including the oomph of, say, Tanelorn.  Songs like Wheel of Time are why I got into metal in the first place:  metal bands write ++entire albums++ and not just a hit single that'll start collecting dust in a few months.  How many power metal albums have utterly dominating, climatic epics to close their albums?  *cough* Rhapsody *cough*    Some things about Blind Guardian just leave me wondering.  Why, for instance, in a 25-year career have they only released 9 albums?  Dragon Guardian, for example (and you can see why:  the band must obviously worship BLIND Guardian), released 3 full-lengths in the period between May 15th and August 18th of 2010.  (I have the middle of these 3 albums and it's so fun!)  That's three albums in less than 100 days (total of 6 albums since their formation in 2006).  How the hell...???  But they do it like that over there in Japan, I guess - just look at how fast Galneryus has risen to the international stage.      But I digress.  Blind Guardian (once again, like Sonata Arctica) refuses to give us a bad song.  Sure, some hold my interest less than others, but that is the case on 99% of the albums out there for 99% of the people out there, right?  As it turns out, Curse My Name and Control the Divine are instrumentally two of my snores on the album, but are concerned with John Milton's Paradise Lost - the \\"epic [poem] to end all epics,\\" as it is said, and the poem \\"to justify the ways of God to men,\\" as Milton himself says.  As a student of British literature, I'll tune into these songs despite the mediocre composition of the instruments.  I have yet to confirm my initial reactions, but I think Curse My Name \\"takes place\\" before the fall while Control the Divine\\" could be a number of fixed moments in time where the thousands of devils ask themselves why Satan is the leader of the hellish army as opposed to one of the others present at the council.  Oh well - I'll have this album for plenty of long enough to answer this.      You should too.  Blind Guardian is an essential band in power metal.  Why?  They're innovative:  They play speed metal (not uncommon), but they are bards (uncommon).  Stratovarius is essential; they're innovative:  neoclassical melodic/power metal with the most thought-provoking lyrics I've ever heard.  Gamma Ray/Helloween are essential for their merging comedy/entertainment/cheeriness with a metal sound.  Sonata Arctica are essential for their reaching deep into the listener's emotions.  And so on and so on.  And this album does present us with an orchestra.  I think that we all need to hear these bands when they incorporate an orchestra (even Dimmu without ICS Vortex and Hellhammer) if for nothing else to keep up to speed with what's going on in today's metal world.  Is this the perfect metal album?  Certainly not.  Perfect Blind Guardian album?  Not that either.  A great listen nonetheless?  Sure!  (How about a Blind Guardian + Symphony X project for a 65-minute concept album on something from Ancient Greece?  Like Demons & Wizards?  Well I can dream, can't I?)

It is something much different, and a good deal better than any of those (with the arguable exception of The Curve).<br /><br />Olive Penderghast (Zombieland's Emma Stone) is your typical invisible high schooler, noticed only by her best friend Rhiannon (Phil of the Future's Alyson Michalka). Things change when Olive, spinning a very tall tale for Rhiannon in the bathroom as an excuse to get out of a camping trip, is overheard by Marianne (Hairspray's Amanda Bynes), the head of the ultra-Christian contingent of the student body, who starts the rumor mill flying. Olive decides to adopt the \\"if you can't beat 'em\\" attitude and dressing the part (without ever actually doing anything actually, you know, salacious). All is well and good until she confesses the lie to Brandon (Cougar Town's Dan Byrd), the school's only gay teen, who hatches a plan; since Olive is not only straight, but now seen as a tramp, telling people he's been with her could give his image a makeover, too. She agrees to help, but the consequences are much father-reaching than she could ever have anticipated.<br /><br />The synopsis doesn't get you anywhere close to the great cast this movie supports. Stanley Tucci and Patricia Clarkson play Olive's parents (Dill and Rosemary, no less), and are the best part about the movie every time they're on the screen. Thomas Haden Church and Lisa Kudrow play a husband-and-wife English teacher and guidance counselor at Olive's high school. Cam Gigandet takes a break from doing below-average horror flicks to take a turn as Marianne's boyfriend, a 22-year-old who's repeating senior year for the fourth time. This is good stuff indeed from a casting perspective, but how's the script? It's hard to believe this is Bert Royal's first feature; this is as sharp and as witty as the John Hughes movies Olive idolizes. (The Quizno's guy, and nice product placement there by the by, is as funny as the ball-waxer in Gutterballs, and that's saying something for a guy who has two lines; for the record it's Rawson Thurber, director of The Mysteries of Pittsburgh.)<br /><br />Sure, the movie has its problems. It's a teen comedy John Hughes-style, of course it has its problems. There are a number of times when the script could have gone in very interesting directions and instead chose to go the conventional route (especially where Marianne is concerned; she could have been an interesting character, but instead remains a one-dimensional object of audience ridicule), for example. And when did the clause in Patricia Clarkson's contract that required a nude scene in every movie run out? Someone's got to get that renewed. But for all that, it's smart, it's funny, and you should give it a look. *** am Gigandet takes a break from doing below-average horror flicks to take a turn as Marianne's boyfriend, a 22-year-old who's repeating senior year for the fourth time. I agree with the other reviewer who said they need an art book for this movie. Truly, if you see no other movie this year, go see Legend of the Guardians in 3D. This is one of those memories that stays with you forever. One of the GOOD ones.

B003UYUR10|1.0|A lot of money went into this production and it DID find its audience among the masses who like eye candy. But frankly this film put me to sleep. Literally. 'Prince of Persia' displays the same faults that so many effects laden movies transfered from other mediums have. Movies such as this may satisfy the crowd that wants to see it's favorite graphic novel or video game on the big screen, but for the rest of us, it is a big bore. The producers no doubt have satisfied its audience but for the rest of us, those who want story, depth of character, and a thoughtful drama, we have to look elsewhere.   'Prince of Persia' makes the mistake that so many of this genre does: The content does not fit the medium. Allow me to explain.    The educated film goer wants more out of the film experience than fast cuts, CGI, flashy visuals, loud audio and pseudo 3D. This works fine for mediums like video games and graphic novels, but film is an altogether different experience. 'Prince of Persia' could have been a good film if the producers developed the property as an addition to the mythos of the Prince of Persia video game universe. It could have been an origins tale like 'Batman Begins', or an historical version like 'Pirates of the Caribbean', or a character driven story like 'Iron Man'. But it wasn't. Disney took the easy way out and gave mass audiences what it was most familiar with - a cinematic video game. A film that will make a lot of money but will be forgotten in time. Not a film that you will go back to years from now. Not a film that will be celebrated as a classic of its genre like 'Superman' or the other films I mentioned here.    As for the film itself, the buffed up Jake Gyllenhaal looks amazingly like the current  graphic depiction of the main character. He does the character justice. By that I mean he acts like a monotone character out of a video game and that is not necessarily a bad thing. Gemma Arterton's portrayal as Tamina is not very good. It is probably not her fault that her character's behavior is inconsistent and that her lines are terrible. Ben Kingsley is a good as always. You just know that he is evil the first time you see him in this film.    The attempt to duplicate the running, jumping, and swinging of the main character of the video game is too  fake CGI to believe. The director tried to duplicate 'parkour', as used the much better films such as Casino Royale' and 'District 13',  but not very successfully.     The ending of the film is confusing and bears the mark of writers who did not know how to logically end the story. But I guess it looks good on the big screen.    Overall, this film deserves a rental and then pass it on the dustbin of what-might-have-been films.

The Hunger Games pulled me into Katniss's troubled world, and Catching Fire gave me a taste of Collins' incredible story telling and original plot. I give the previous 2 books 5 stars. They were incredible. However, I found this one heartbreakingly disappointing. First off, I want to say that the story was relatively unexpected me. I was anticipating more action, better character development, deep delving into human nature and personalities, a thorough exploration of morality and humanity, a more mentally stable Katniss (she was deranged throughout most of the book!), a better portrayed Gale (as one of the strongest and bravest characters, he deserved more respect. He was treated like a monster), less unnecessary deaths (Boggs? Finnick? PRIM?), a stronger plot. I can summarize it in a couple of sentences: Katniss is almost over the edge the entire book, is forced into the Mockingjay position, she is weak and frail and attempts training, Peeta, rescued from the Capitol, hates Katniss and is crazy and restrained, Katniss goes to the Capitol to knock some heads to kill Snow, and then most of her allies die, and Prim dies, leaving her collapsable and crazed. Next, there is an maddeningly vague explanation that she slowly recovers, though emotionally scarred for life, gets knocked up by Peeta for real, has 2 kids and forgets about Gale altogether.   My response: WTF????  The story was dull and boring at first (like Catching Fire, but at least it made up for it later) and failed to meet my expectations as the story progressed. The other stories had so much substance, this book hardly had any meat on its bones. The lack of Collins' usual wry humor and better character analysis was also a glaring error. Peeta is simply turned insane and is unreachable, and Katniss hardly tried getting him back. Katniss. Another thing. Iwas hurt and confused, way back in Catching Fire, when after leaving Gale for the Quarter Quell, she simply says \\"I'm not going back to him. Might as well flirt with Peeta with the time I have left.\\"   Katniss. You're breaking my restraint to call you a \\"garden utensil\\".... or the thing that rhymes with \\"boar\\"... for now I'll call your shallow attitude as you being a 'tease'. So, since Gale is gone, you just go to Peeta and forget about him? You're right. You are acting like a coward. And being selfish. I thought Katniss was being noble when she said she was unfriendly in the first book, where I found her compassionate and brave. By the 2nd book, my opinion of her was slightly weaning because of her altering attitudes and easy and betraying transfers from Peeta to Gale. (make up your mind already!) Now, I realize that by some point in book 2, she became really unlikable. I still felt sympathy for her dead love ones, but somewhat less for her emotional state.   Gale. Another thing. He is portrayed as heartless and crude. No. No, no, no. Gale, based on previous actions, is noble, self-sacrificing, and brave. AND handsome. He and Katniss are very similar, but he appears more likable to me. Sure, he may be harsh on his enemies, but at least his morals are clear. Katniss is a jumbled-up mess who can't make good decisions anymore. I suppose her reaction to everything was realistic, but I think Collins should have changed the story so we could ultimately see the stronger Everdeen fighting for freedom, justice, and protection of her loved ones. Instead they all die off, leaving her mad. And I can tell that Collins spent a lot of time on the first part of the book, but the ending with Coin's death and the epilogue were rushed. I'm almost sure of it. It was irritating to see so many questions unanswered. And Katniss and Peeta. Collins found her love triangle to difficult to untangle, so she took a shortcut and made Katniss prefer Peeta by relating Gale to Prim's death. I think that Gale should've given a sincere apology, he's not evil, and Katniss should grudgingly forgive him, and have their relationship grow. (Yes, I am Team Gale. But who isn't a sucker for 2 best friends falling in love? No one cares that it's cliche, it's still fitting.)   I did not hate this book in general. Collins is still an amazing write, and her plots are weaved with intricate detail (FOB song reference) and I still partially enjoyed the book: the action scenes, the actually touching or happier scenes, and the unique twists in the plot as well. You should buy it (used. or get it from the library), never leave a series unfinished if you really did like previous ones, those questions hanging around you will never go away. It was good, but failed to meet Collins' standards. And the ending was too blurred. I miss the days of excited anticipation of reading this final book of the trilogy, and now I am sort of wallowing in the hollowness I feel inside... though I'm sure later the highlights of the book I failed to recognize will come to me soon. I went into this flick expecting something that was absolutely unwatchable, and what I got was a film that (had it been from any other director) would have been regarded as an entertaining, effective horror flick. Romero however suffers from being  judged by other standards. But what of the film? Just some random thoughts there.    In SURVIVAL Romero picks up temporally where DIARY OF THE DEAD left off since the story takes place approximately 6 days after the events depicted in that film. But thematically it harkens back to both NIGHT and LAND OF THE DEAD. At the finale of LAND OF THE DEAD his zombies pull a Garbo,  stalking off into the darkness, wanting nothing more than to be left alone. Unfortunately this raises the question of how they can possibly separate themselves from human beings when they depend upon them for their food source. In SURVIVAL Romero seems to be laying a foundation that will deal with this troublesome matter. But I found the greatest references in this film to be to NOTLD because it was in that seminal film that Romero first presented his human beings as having the potential to  be greater monsters than the zombies who were only fulfilling a new biological imperative to eat their own kind. The human beings on the other hand were engaged in meaningless struggles for equally meaningless power from the first moment that Ben and Harry faced off in that deserted farm house-- Power struggles that actually caused their  deaths. SURVIVAL's entire plot is built around the struggle for power between the patriarchs of two Irish clans on isolated Plum Island, and, as in NOTLD, this struggle forpower causes everything to end tragically--although in the case of SURVIVAL it also serves to set up another potential film.    Romero seems to be saying that while his zombies can evolve, we human beings are doomed to repeat our self-destructive behavior endlessly. If we aren't engaged in power struggles, doggedly determined to prove ourselves right no matter what the cost, then we're sadistic rednecks merrily engaged in torturing the undead just for fun--another recurring theme in his flix that has become magnified over the course of the series.  It seems a very pessimistic view of America (if not the world) but a quick peek at American politics serves to re-enforce that view. 0399536108|5.0|The Art of Non Conformity is an incredible book. Chris wants you to set the rules for your life, make a difference, have fun doing it, and take full responsibility for your life. The review that follows is an honest assessment of what the book is, and what it isn't.<br /><br />What it is<br />Chris Guillebeau marches to a different beat and designs his life the way he wants it to be. In the first chapter he tells you his goal in writing the book:<br /><br />\\"The mission is to support a full-scale revolution with a simple underlying message: You don't have to live your life the way other people expect you to\\"<br /><br />Chris speaks from the heart, and although the story woven into the book is slow at times, it's very interesting. How can a thirty-something be more than half-way to visiting every country in the world?!<br /><br />I believe in the power of success stories, because then our own definitions for what is possible get redefined instantly (i.e. \\"If he can do it, why can't I?). This book is a great exercise is thinking outside of the box, or more precisely, thinking exactly the opposite from everything you're supposed to think.<br /><br />What seems like the biggest regret of his life, paying $32,000 for graduate school, turns out to be a gift. Throughout the book, Chris details how to get a similar education, make more of an impact, and save a ton of cash. The self proclaimed introvert follows a life path that many people dream of, and takes you on a journey with him into his world. He doesn't suggest foregoing grad school for those who must go for their dream career, but if grad school is just a cherry on top of an undergrad career to \\"pad your resume\\", there are other ways of being successful.<br /><br />The Art of Non Conformity is a manifesto of sorts that will allow you to begin to think like a leader. One of the greatest parts of the book is where Chris talks about building a tribe of dedicated followers. Lady Gaga has them, the Harry Potter Series has them, and so does every successful idea in history. He says that 1000 loyal fans will support all of your dreams financially, which I agree with. One thousand dedicated people focused on one cause can do a lot.<br /><br />There is tremendous power in having so many people completely dedicated to something. But so many of us allow ourselves to be the followers, rather than the leader. And you can be both. Why not be part of a small army somewhere, and lead your own movement at the same time? It's all outlined in this book.<br /><br />What it isn't<br />It reminds me a lot of the Four Hour Work Week, but with a different agenda. While Tim gives the reader specific tools and strategies for building the business that will free your time and give you the mobility you need, The AONC is more of a book designed to get you thinking in terms of mobility and freedom, focusing on building your army, giving back to those in need, and making an impact.<br /><br />To put it simply, someone who thinks in terms of changing the world, as outlined per AONC, will certainly need the tools outlined in a book like 4HWW: [[ASIN:0307465357 The 4-Hour Workweek, Expanded and Updated: Expanded and Updated, With Over 100 New Pages of Cutting-Edge Content.]] The two go hand in hand. There may have been one or two mentions of wordpress, but beyond that there's not much tech-talk in here.<br /><br />The only criticism I have of the book is that the resources for readers new to the web are a bit thin. For those who have been blogging a while, this may be the time to change course, develop your muse through which you will change the world, and get to it. But for novices, it would take at least one or two more books to get to that point. Even a quick and dirty outline of blogging platforms, books to read, people to learn from, and other resources would have been enough. But that's really a small criticism.<br /><br />This is a literary reset button for all those not currently following their dreams. What this book is trying to get at is that your dreams are possible to achieve. Take hold of the reigns and make a difference.<br /><br />Highlights from the Book<br />It's great to see that even a world famous blogger and writer gets scared sometimes. It bridges the gap psychologically between someone who is living an amazing life, and someone who is reading about it at home, when that life seems thousands of miles away.<br /><br />One of the principles Chris talks about is acknowledging that fear is in front of you, and facing it anyways. In chapter 3, he writes:<br /><br />\\"A few things I am currently afraid of:<br />-I'm only on chapter 3. Will I ever finish writing this book?<br />-What if it sucks? What if I get bad reviews? (Or worse, what if no one pays any attention?)<br />-I'm afraid that people will think I am faking it\\"<br /><br />What I am trying to get at is that everyone, even the guy writing the book you are reading, runs into mental roadblocks. Suddenly our excuses don't have as much power. Bust through your doubts and lead your followers to victory.<br />And make no mistake, you will have your own doubts even as you read this book? \\"But he doesn't have X, or I still have to deal with Y.\\"  You get the sense that Chris's biggest goal is leaving a legacy beyond himself. When those sort of dreams are truly important to you, you begin to take the sort of steps that make your life extraordinary, as demonstrated by Chris's impact already.<br /><br />My favorite chapter is called \\"Graduate School vs. the Blogosphere.\\" As you can surmise, he outlines how starting a real life business online is more effective for you and your wallet long term. Because once he understood how to \\"be a good student\\", he excelled. But that didn't improve his chances of success in the real world. It was only his experience as an entrepreneur, writer, and non-conformist that allowed him the chance to make a real impact.<br /><br />He even compares the results from his thesis vs. those of his blog:<br />Feedback from people after working on thesis: \\" (the words) 'Good Job' from 3 people.\\"<br />Feedback from writing for Art of Non Conformity: \\"Thousands of positive comments from all over the world.\\"<br /><br />Chris is very honest in his writing. The best part about this is that he's right. Kind of puts things in perspective, doesn't it?<br /><br />As an aside, I actually ran into a similar situation while in school. I can remember being called to write 20 pages on a big environmental issue after researching the issue and coming up with a solution. Out of the 20 pages, I devoted a half page to say that one solution could be to have a dedicated blog, facebook page, and twitter account dedicated to raising awareness for the issue (this was spring 2009). My teacher wrote in red ink, \\"I don't know what this is supposed to be...\\"<br /><br />Takeaways<br />The Art of Non Conformity is about re-framing your reasons for making a difference. Instead of focusing on \\"what can I get?\\", we should focus on \\"how can I get paid to help as many people as possible and make a difference?\\" And if you don't have an answer, that's ok. As another reviewer on amazon put it,<br />\\"It is a primer that I think anyone who is considering the question \\"What do I do?\\" should read.\\"<br /><br />So how big can you dream? How big of an impact do you want to have in the end? Questions like this lead people towards amazing lives. Some remain unsung heroes, but with today's tools and technologies, everyone has the platform to be un-conventional and lead a life that you design.<br /><br />So what are you \\"not conforming\\" to exactly, once you read this book? Well, a few things.<br />First, other peoples' expectations. No one is going to have as big a dream as you do for your life. Visiting every country in the world by the time you're 35? To say that Chris is not easily persuaded from achieving his goals would be an understatement.<br /><br />Second, gatekeepers. These are the people in the way of what you want. Chris puts it bluntly: they tell you what choices you have, rather than you defining them for yourself. The book give great strategies for handling these obstacles that are inevitably in the way of any major goal. Again, many of these people may not believe that it's possible for you to do what you want to do. But keep going.<br /><br />Lastly, your own expectations. Redefine what's normal. Help a cause 10X bigger than yourself. Build an army. Think big. Be an entrepreneur. Travel. See the world. Meet with the movers and shakers in your niche. B003TVMIDO|4.0|\\"Suddenly your 30 and there's so much more to do\\"/\\"Life begins at 40\\"-John Lennon<br /><br />Released as part of the celebration of what would have been John Lennon's 70th birthday, the \\"John Lennon Signature Box\\" gathers the 5 solo studio albums Lennon made between 1970-1975, Lennon's recordings that were exclusive to his singles, John and Yoko's 1980 album \\"Double Fantasy\\", the posthumous release \\"Milk and Honey\\" and a disc of previously unreleased demos/alternate takes from 1970-1971 in a handsomely designed box.<br /><br />Missing from this set are the trio of avant garde albums that Lennon and Ono recorded together prior to 1970--\\"Two Virgins\\", \\"The Wedding Album\\" and \\"Life with the Lions\\". Also missing is \\"Live Peace in Toronto\\" and while we get \\"Double Fantasy\\" we don't get the \\"Double Fantasy Stripped\\" disc which features the album without the overdubs which is a missed opportunity and it SHOULD have been included but both EMI and Ono want you to shell out the extra bucks for that album (which means if you buy this set you'll have two discs with the original \\"Double Fantasy\\" mix).<br /><br />The remastered sound done by the same team that did The Beatles remasters from last year are a marked improvement over the original late 80's CD releases for the first 5 albums. These will sound different from the 2000 reissue of those albums because those were remixes. These are the original mixes that Lennon prepared. The remixes had their merit as well since often the remixing improved the overall clarity of some albums (Particularly \\"Mind Games\\") but they were flawed as they were heavily compressed (which makes things louder and sacrifices the dynamics, i.e., the range between the quietest and loudest moments on an album. It's the dynamics that often give the music it's power for example without the dynamic build up of sound from \\"A Day in the Life\\", the song would lose much of its power). They also suffered from harsh peak limiting which causes digital distortion.<br /><br />For these new remasters the EMI team went back to the original mastertapes, cleaned them up, eliminated any imperfections due to edits and used every device they could to come as close to the sound of the master tapes as possible. The results are better low end, more detailed and powerful bass and drums and improved clarity in the vocals and instrument separation. Noise reduction was used minimally (the overuse of which was a flaw with the U.S. and UK original CDs of these albums particularly for \\"Mind Games\\" and \\"Imagine\\").<br /><br />\\"Double Fantasy\\" and \\"Milk & Honey\\" were NOT worked on by the Abbey Road/EMI team but were remastered by George Marino for this set. While they are an improvement over the 2001 remasters for both albums,I don't think that they show quite as much improvement over the original CD issues of these albums. In a comparison to both \\"DF\\" and \\"M&H\\" 2001 editions and these, the ones included here don't sound signficantly different--not quite as loud perhaps but not Marino's best work either. Both suffer from hard limiting and compression and do not sound as good as the EMI/Abbey Road work done on the first five albums and singles on the first bonus disc.<br /><br />The downside is that the bonus tracks that were included on the 2000 remixes aren't always here and none of them are attached to the original disc (some may prefer these that way although I don't since some of the bonus track editions were a bit odd to me). Some of the songs (such as some of the singles like \\"Power to the People\\") are included on the two disc singles/demos disc included here. The 13 previously unreleased tracks on the second bonus disc are quite good some of which are radically different arrangements (\\"I Don't Want to Be a Soldier\\"), some subtly so (\\"Mother\\" with guitar on the track as well as piano, drums and bass)and the veresion of \\"Serve Yourself\\" is superior to the one on the \\"Lennon Anthology\\" in my opinion. While \\"India\\" and the demo for \\"Nobody ToldMe\\" are quite good as well. I do wish that the unfinished \\"Now and Then\\" (which the rest of The Beatles briefly thought about completing for the \\"Anthology\\" series)had been included.<br /><br />The packaging is quite nice and includes a booklet with an essay, reproductions of original artwork and lyric sheets about Lennon, brief essays by Yoko, Julian Lennon and Sean Lennon. There's also a card for internet content included as well and a lithograph which is in a drawer at the bottom of the box. The CDs themselves are housed (without protective sleeves I might add--which is a negative given how much you may pay for this set)cardboard sleeves similar in design to The Beatles remasters. The drawer is not the best idea as the lithograph can easily become stuck and, quite frankly, it seems unnecessary and silly as the litho will fit on top of the CDs in the box proper.<br /><br />The booklets are quite nice and include an essay on the making of the album as well as the original lyrics (except for \\"Rock 'n' Roll\\")and credits.<br /><br />Bottom line: Will you like this set? If you prefer the remixes probably not but, if you want Lennon's original album mixes restored and presented with top notch sound and attention to detail, you will. The \\"bonus disc\\" with the demos/alternate takes aren't essential but they are nice to have and compliment the \\"Lennon Anthology\\" from a decade ago.<br /><br />These sound exceptional although, like most remasters, there are some flaws one could nitpick about. These reissues beat the original CD issues of the original mixes. Overall this is a nice set and would make an excellent gift for Lennon/Beatles fans particularly the latter who enjoyed the Beatles remasters.<br /><br />It's sad that we'll never know what Lennon had to say about being 70. The booklets are quite nice and include an essay on the making of the album as well as the original lyrics (except for \\"Rock 'n' Roll\\")and credits.    Bottom line: Will you like this set? If you prefer the remixes probably not but, if you want Lennon's original album mixes restored and presented with top notch sound and attention to detail, you will. The \\"bonus disc\\" with the demos/alternate takes aren't essential but they are nice to have and compliment the \\"Lennon Anthology\\" from a decade ago.     These sound exceptional although, like most remasters, there are some flaws one could nitpick about. These reissues beat the original CD issues of the original mixes. Overall this is a nice set and would make an excellent gift for Lennon/Beatles fans particularly the latter who enjoyed the Beatles remasters.      It's sad that we'll never know what Lennon had to say about being 70. Recommended.

0316069485|2.0|Not recommended. Much as I like Michael Connelly's books, this is one of his least successful efforts. Bosch and Haller together are less than either alone. Supporting figures are for the most part either lifeless or falsely animated when the scene requires them to be active or engaged. The ex-wives, children and half-brothers never come together in a credible mix. The whole thing comes across as formulaic and programmatic, almost as if Connelly set himself the challenge of taking all the major surviving characters from previous Bosch and Haller novels and layering them into a single tale; the attempt gets a B for effort and D for achievement. It doesn't help that the bad guy is too one-dimensional to be of more than passing interest, and the did-he-or-didn't-he premise of the tale is never given a chance to be more than the anchor position in a battle of strategies. His defense attorney gets at least two dimensions, which is still not enough. Nobody gets three, but a judge, an emotionally damaged witness and the leader of a surveillance operation come closer than the others.     Let me offer special condemnation of the author's jarring use of both first person and third person narrative styles to keep the Haller and Bosch narratives separate. There's a reason beginning writers are encouraged not to do this.    No reason to keep beating a dead horse. I see the first dozen reviewers think more highly of this effort than I do. I'm glad they got a book they liked. I'll hope for more from the next one.

With the ability to view the film in 3 key forms, the original motion capture with the actors in the soundstage, the \\"template\\" which is the inbetween of the motion capture and the final CG, and of course the final version, as well as Picture in Picture with interviews and commentary, this special feature is one that is only possible with the advanced storage space of Blu-Ray. B003HLM2QO|2.0|The Ozzman cometh again in 2010 with new album Scream, an album I had put down as an essential purchase for 2010.  But I was wrong.  This latest offering from the legendary bat biter is kind of like when your dad goes out wearing a pair of  length cargo pants:  it might be cool for someone else twenty or thirty years younger but just doesn't sit right with a bloke of this age.  And just as I'd hope my dad would rip `em off and put on a pair of decent trousers the likes of which he used to wear and I respected him for, same goes for Ozzy here.  Act your age man!  Whoever is behind the choice of musical direction on this album really needs to have a good hard look at themselves.  Ozzy, you are not the future defining/inspiring nu-age metal kid, you are the effin' Prince Of Darkness for f's sake!  You have inspired legions of metal bands and fans already, no need to keep on trying to keep on doing it, nor is there any need to cash in on what some might perceive as the flavour of the month.<br /><br />This album abounds in what some might call \\"studio trickery\\" to a ridiculous degree which makes one ponder if Ozzy is still actually really capable of delivering the goods vocally.  It abounds in over production.  It abounds in the chaotic aural clutter of nu-metal/industrial metal, swirling in noise and confusion to the suffering of decent melody.  There are a few exceptions, notably Scream, Life Won't Wait and Time which sound much more like the kind of stuff one hopes to hear from Ozzy, harking back a bit more to the traditional metal sound we all know him for.  And despite its modern style Digging Me Down isn't too bad, but in comparison to stuff from the likes of No More Tears (or any album pre-Black Rain for that matter) or classic Black Sabbath this is not a song Ozzy fans would chose to put on a \\"best of\\" collection.<br /><br />Unfortunately despite some good solos from Gus G here and there, the rest of the album is pretty average, run of the mill to insipid stuff really, with the final track I Love You All perhaps being the ultimate example.  So what we have with this album is a true living legend desperately trying to sound modern and fresh, wanting to prove to the world that he is still an inspiring and relevant force in music today, but coming across like that embarrassing dad in the  cargo pants that you walk three paces behind when you go down the street.the final track I Love You All perhaps being the ultimate example.  So what we have with this album is a true living legend desperately trying to sound modern and fresh, wanting to prove to the world that he is still an inspiring and relevant force in music today, but coming across like that embarrassing dad in the  cargo pants that you walk three paces behind when you go down the street.

B003MZ0I84|3.0|Mutt Lange has a knack for making it all sound effortless. He knows the formula and can turn out tunes with that \\"fresh from the farm\\" feel. This is a good and bad thing. For years he spent glossing up the hits for Shania Twain but you don't have to look far into the Twain cataloge to see that the content is about as deep as a puddle.   \\"Man! I Feel Like A Woman!\\" \\"Don't Be Stupid, You Know I Love You!\\"  Not exactly profound word play. But the man is a radio friendly hit-machine with those not searching too deep for quality.   But, I digress. Lange didn't write the songs on Hands All Over but he might as well have. With the exception of a few tunes, everything has been done before and done better. HAO is really all over the place. The lack of any cohesion isn't the problem, though nothing fits together. The downside for me was the overwhelming corniness.     \\"Misery\\" has a catchy hook, perfectly crafted for radio but something screams \\"This Love 2010\\". I like it for what it is - radio friendly.     \\"Get Back In My Life\\" is Squeeze-revisited. Squeeze circa 1985. Bouncy, lively and only missing the cheeky delivery of Glenn Tilford.     \\"Out Of Goodbyes\\", featuring Lady Antebellum, starts out like a weeper and ends in a wimper. What should be a harmonious blending of vocals turns monotone fast.     \\"Hands All Over\\" tries really hard to be an anthem. The lyrics are really cheesy and, diehards might not agree, it lacks any sex appeal M5 fans come to expect. With a title like that, I sort of expected the signature \\"obscure dirty\\".   This isn't a hand on a thigh. It's a pat on the head.     \\"Last Chance\\"...seriously? The lyrics are just oh-so-bad.   \\"I bet that he could give your everything/Stability and diamonds rings/All the things I do not have/I know you can't handle that.\\"     \\"I Can't Lie\\" is growing on me but it sounds dated. I've heard this before, much like \\"Get Back In My Life\\". Only this time, it's more Motown then Brit pop. I could hear Smokey Robinson singingthis. In 1965.     \\"Never Gonna Leave This Bed\\" could've been recorded by Journey. Of course, it would've been a little faster and far more passionate.      \\"Stutter\\" sounds like a novelty tune in the same vein as \\"Stacy's Mom\\" by Bowling For Soup. I expect a video of all the boys, leaning on a wall, snapping their finger in unison while wearing Ray Bans and leg warmers.     \\"Give A Little More\\" is perhaps the best song on the album. Adam Levine's voice is flawless. This is HAO's \\"Makes Me Wonder\\". A little funky, a little sexy and alot catchy.     \\"No Curtain Call\\" (available on the deluxe edition) is my second favorite song on the album. No sure why this wasn't part of the original 12 tunes selected because it should be. It's far more captivating than alot of the songs that made the original cut.     The acoustic verison of \\"Misery\\" (available on the deluxe edition) is a huge letdown. This is where Lange's production comes in. Acoustic usually feels a little more free-form, a little edgier. This acoustic tale doesn't feel acoustic at all; like it's not instruments but a generic digital recording. A music bed you would hear in a commercial for Pringles.     Lastly, the cover of Alicia Keys \\"If I Ain't Got You\\" is lovely and safe. Levine's voice is in fine form. It's a straight forward cover. No special M5 infused flavor. I'm not a Keys fan, so I have to say M5's cover is far superior to her yelling/singing.     All and all...a bit of a letdown. There are a few sparkling gems but missing is that signature M5 smirk. Like your privy to the inside joke. With \\"Songs About Jane\\" and \\"It Won't Be Soon Before Long\\", I felt like we got into the mind of the band and for 45-50 minutes, hung out. HAO is all formula with little payoff.    3 stars for Adam Levine's voice and the few tunes that deliver the happy ending.
